widget-instance-OUP_Article_FullText_Widget\">\n        <div class=\"module-widget\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget-items\" data-widgetname=\"ArticleFulltext\">\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                    <h2 scrollto-destination=117975996 id=\"117975996\" class=\"abstract-title\" >ABSTRACT</h2>\r\n<section class=\"abstract\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">We report the discovery of a new low-redshift galaxy-scale gravitational lens, identified from a systematic search of publicly available MUSE observations. The lens galaxy, 2MASXJ04035024\u22120239275, is a giant elliptical at <em>z</em>=\u00a00.066\u00a004 with a velocity dispersion of \u03c3\u00a0=\u00a0314\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>. The lensed source has a redshift of 0.191\u00a065 and forms a pair of bright images on either side of the lens centre. The Einstein radius is 1.5\u2009arcsec, projecting to 1.8\u00a0kpc, which is just one quarter of the galaxy effective radius. After correcting for an estimated 19\u2009per\u00a0cent dark matter contribution, we find that the stellar mass-to-light ratio from lensing is consistent with that expected for a Milky Way initial mass function (IMF). Combining the new system with three previously studied low-redshift lenses of similar \u03c3, the derived mean mass excess factor (relative to a Kroupa IMF) is \u27e8\u03b1\u27e9\u00a0=\u00a01.09\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.08. With all four systems, the intrinsic scatter in \u03b1 for massive elliptical galaxies can be limited to &lt;0.32, at 90\u2009per\u00a0cent confidence.</p></section>                        <div class=\"article-metadata-panel clearfix\"></div>\r\n<div class=\"kwd-group\"><a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;gravitational lensing: strong&quot;\">gravitational lensing: strong</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: elliptical and lenticular, cD&quot;\">galaxies: elliptical and lenticular, cD</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: stellar content&quot;\">galaxies: stellar content</a></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=117975998 id=\"117975998\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec1>1 INTRODUCTION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The initial mass function (IMF) is fundamental to understanding galaxy formation and evolution, as well as to interpreting observed properties (e.g. estimating stellar masses). Within the different star-forming environments in the Milky Way (MW), the stellar IMF is well constrained, and approximately invariant (Bastian, Covey &amp; Meyer <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib2\" data-open=\"bib2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Offner et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>). Deviations towards a flatter IMF have been reported for some resolved ultrafaint Local Group dwarfs (Geha et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">For galaxies beyond the Local Group, resolved studies are not possible, and the IMF must be inferred from the integrated light and/or gravitational mass tracers. Broadly the observational techniques fall into two categories. The first method infers the stellar population via high signal-to-noise spectroscopy. The strength of gravity sensitive absorption lines is measured by fitting detailed stellar population synthesis templates. The second method indirectly measures the stellar population by comparing a stellar mass-to-light ratio (<em>M</em>/<em>L</em>) measured from stellar dynamics or strong lensing, to a reference <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> from a fixed IMF stellar population model. Studies independently utilizing both techniques have found evidence for an increasingly \u2018heavy\u2019 IMF (more measured mass than a fixed IMF model predicts) in the most massive Early Type Galaxiess (ETGs) (e.g. Treu et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib43\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib43\" data-open=\"bib43\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Cappellari et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>; Conroy &amp; van Dokkum <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012b</a>; La Barbera et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The mass measurements from gravitational lensing require careful treatment to disentangle the contributions of stellar mass and dark matter (DM). For example, Treu et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib43\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib43\" data-open=\"bib43\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) analysed lenses at <em>z</em> \u223c \u20090.2 by combining lensing and stellar kinematics to constrain the parameters of a two-component mass model. This approach involves several assumptions (spherical geometry, constant stellar mass-to-light ratio, etc.). In contrast, <em>nearby</em> (<em>z</em> \u2009\u2264 \u20090.1) lenses offer a geometry in which the Einstein radius (<em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub>) is reached at smaller physical radii and hence probes the dense stellar-dominated core. In such cases the relative uncertainty from DM is minimized, as the ratio of dark to stellar matter is reduced and \u2018pure\u2019 lensing constraints on the stellar mass can be obtained (i.e. with no additional information from stellar kinematics).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">At present, the best studied <em>z</em>\u2009 &lt; \u20090.1 lensing ellipticals are those discovered from the SINFONI Nearby Elliptical Lens Locator Survey (SNELLS) (Smith, Lucey &amp; Conroy <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>). This targeted approach used an integral field unit (IFU) to search for background emitters behind massive elliptical galaxies, finding SNL-1 (<em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.031) and SNL-2 (<em>z</em>=\u00a00.052), and \u2018rediscovering\u2019 the previously known lens SNL-0 (<em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.034) (Smith et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib39\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib39\" data-open=\"bib39\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>; Smith &amp; Lucey <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib38\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib38\" data-open=\"bib38\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>). The SNELLS galaxies yielded lensing masses in strong disagreement with \u2018heavy\u2019 IMFs for massive ETGs, instead measuring <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> consistent with an MW-like IMF (Kroupa <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>), both in low-resolution ground-based (Smith et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>), and in high-resolution space-based observations (Collier, Smith &amp; Lucey <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">With only three galaxies, the possibility that the SNELLS sample <em>by chance</em> was drawn from the \u2018tail\u2019 of an intrinsically broad distribution in IMFs cannot be ruled out. As such, with low-number statistics, increasing the sample size is essential to investigate further this conclusion, and to test whether these lenses are representative of the parent population.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Discovering low-redshift lenses is challenging due to the high surface brightness of the massive foreground galaxy. While SNELLS pre-selected high-probability lenses using velocity dispersion measurements, an alternative approach exploiting large multi-IFU surveys like SAMI and MaNGA (Bryant et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>; Bundy et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib5\" data-open=\"bib5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>) has recently yielded new systems (Smith <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib37\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib37\" data-open=\"bib37\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>; Talbot et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib42\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib42\" data-open=\"bib42\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>). A third technique, which we are currently pursuing, is to search for lensed line emitters behind galaxies targeted for other science goals, using data from public archives. Observations made with MUSE (Multi-Unit Spectroscopic Explorer) (Bacon et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>) on the European Southern Observatory (ESO) Very Large Telescope are well suited to this method, due to the very high sensitivity and wide spectral range (4350\u20139300\u2009\u00c5) of the instrument.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this paper, we present the discovery of the first multiply imaged strong gravitational lens from this programme. In Section <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec2\"></span><a href=\"#sec2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">2</a>, we briefly describe the modified lens search process. In Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec3\"></span><a href=\"#sec3\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">3</a> we present the new lens system properties. In Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec4\"></span><a href=\"#sec4\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">4</a> we present lensing mass constraints and the IMF mass excess parameter, and then compare, and combine our results with the SNELLS sample in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec5\"></span><a href=\"#sec5\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">5</a>.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=117976006 id=\"117976006\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2>2 A MUSE ARCHIVAL LENS SEARCH</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Our new lensing system was identified in the course of a systematic search for multiply imaged line emitters behind low-redshift ETGs in public archival MUSE data. A full description of this programme will be presented elsewhere (Collier et\u00a0al., in preparation); here, we briefly summarize the sample selection and main processing steps, to provide context for the new discovery.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">For this lens search, we are analysing public MUSE observations which overlap with luminous nearby galaxies selected from the 2MASS Redshift Survey (Huchra et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib19\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib19\" data-open=\"bib19\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>). We focus our search on targets with redshift 5000\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>\u2009&lt;\u2009<em>cz</em>\u2009&lt;\u200920\u2009000\u2009 km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>, and <em>M<sub>K</sub></em>\u2009&lt;\u2009\u221225.7.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The analysis begins with \u2018Phase 3\u2019 pipeline-reduced products retrieved from the ESO Science Archive. Our primary goal is to detect faint lensed background emitters behind the central regions of nearby ETGs. Before attempting line detection, we therefore process the pipeline-reduced data cubes to subtract the bright stellar foreground, effectively fitting elliptical isophote models to each wavelength channel. The residual cubes are noise-normalized and filtered to suppress instrumental artefacts.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Candidate emission lines are then identified above a significance threshold in the processed residual cubes. Spectra for likely sources are extracted, and redshifts are estimated automatically using <span class=\"small-caps\">marz</span> (Hinton et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>). Finally, the resulting object catalogue is visually inspected, and searched for any spatially separated line emitters with similar redshifts, which are potentially multiply imaged background galaxies.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Although we optimize our search method to detect faint background emission, the first galaxy-scale lens discovered was over 100\u00a0times brighter than our detection limit. Hence we report this system as a \u2018special case\u2019 in this paper.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=117976012 id=\"117976012\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3>3 THE NEW LENS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">2MASXJ04035024\u22120239275 (hereafter J0403) is a luminous (<em>M<sub>K</sub></em>\u00a0=\u00a0\u221225.7) elliptical galaxy at heliocentric redshift <em>z</em><sub>H</sub>=\u00a00.066\u00a055 (Jones et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib20\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib20\" data-open=\"bib20\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>). In imaging from the Pan-STARRS PS1 survey (Chambers et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) J0403 shows a smooth inner light profile, well fitted by a S\u00e9rsic profile with index <em>n</em>\u2009\u00a0\u2248\u00a0\u20094. In the outer regions (radius &gt;\u2009 20\u2009arcsec), some low-surface-brightness (<em>i</em>band \u223c25\u2009mag arcsec<sup>\u22122</sup>) tidal structures are visible. J0403 is a relatively isolated galaxy, with no overdensity of similar-colour objects visible in PS1 imaging. The closest comparable brightness companion (2MASXJ04034531\u22120236595) is a spiral galaxy, fainter by 1.3 mag, with relative velocity +550\u2009km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>, at a projected distance of \u223c210\u00a0kpc (2.7\u00a0arcmin). There are no catalogued clusters with comparable redshift within a radius of 2\u2009\u00a0deg (9.5\u2009\u00a0Mpc).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">J0403 was observed with MUSE as part of a study targeting supernova hosts [ESO programme 098.D-0115(A); PI L. Galbany]. The exposure time was \u223c2400\u2009s, with seeing \u22720.8\u00a0arcsec (estimated full width at half-maximum from compact sources). The MUSE field of view is 1\u00a0\u00d7\u00a01\u00a0arcmin<sup>2</sup> (\u223c80\u00a0\u00d7\u00a080\u00a0kpc<sup>2</sup>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In the MUSE data (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1a</a>), the light profile of J0403 is smooth at small radii, with evidence for the faint tidal features seen in the PS1 imaging. Extracting narrow-band images (Fig.<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1b</a>) highlights a pair of extended background emitters (arcs A and B) either side of the galaxy centre. Strong H\u2009\u03b1, [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>], and H\u2009\u03b2 lines are present in the spectra of both emitters, with the same redshift, <em>z</em><sub>H</sub>=\u00a00.191\u00a065 (Fig.<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a>). The respective velocity offset between A and B is \u226450\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>, providing strong evidence of a common source. When the continuum is subtracted, image A shows slight curvature at low-surface-brightness levels (Fig.<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1c</a>). There is some evidence of a small velocity gradient (\u223c50\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup> peak-to-peak) across arc A, which is mirrored in arc B. A broad-band counterpart to arc A is readily visible in the PS1 imaging, and the counter-arc is recovered after subtraction of a smooth light profile for J0403. The balance of evidence in this case strongly favours a lensing interpretation.<sup><span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn1\" data-open=\"fn1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\">1</a></sup></p>                        <a id=\"117976016\" scrollto-destination=\"117976016\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig1\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 1.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/2/10.1093_mnras_sty1188/2/m_sty1188fig1.jpeg?Expires=1611465988&amp;Signature=Hp1FI9BjKrhNHdJDajrtU~VAmLN99fMKeez3oY~NrRDwdMIyWm34oimbBs9eeBsSVItdsGG68lfx7ZomHeHVT54llapFoFlnlcbxaTqHE8cksBbWkvvKvTZ8hUJReNhekz5uCqSal6Os8D~yvl~Tcx8wEmQLIFHtXkIPparBDC8zxkHrzDa-JZ1mSTIr82SHvltObPaaGvmhkKm7P6F2mUAouUcWBYM6rJec02kq8rzdBMHAi4K882ga5USk0-rEk8atofs-feAZaePXNLfr8A3K9CSqf~EG3k4OFZEfGVsUR3cyqC5aFnrr9tdrNT7XQ6WPkUO5bdAe2hGjtkoAgw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"MUSE data of J0403, within different wavelength ranges (shown in the top right). For our lensing analysis we adopt <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub> as half of the image separation, 1.47\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.03\u00a0arcsec, and extract an aperture spectrum (\u2264<em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub>) of J0403, shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>. The measured heliocentric redshift, <em>z</em><sub>H</sub>=\u00a00.066\u00a004, and velocity dispersion of \u03c3\u00a0=\u00a0314\u00a0\u00b1\u00a05\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup> are found by fitting this central region using <span class=\"small-caps\">ppxf</span> (Cappellari &amp; Emsellem <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2004</a>).</p>                        <a id=\"117976019\" scrollto-destination=\"117976019\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/2/10.1093_mnras_sty1188/2/m_sty1188fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611465988&amp;Signature=S~fJSDrysQMxBUmztFy9zdlNhMHNLw~sSMjm9CUUSYhrPiyMPn-1WKz9pw0cVN82T3rs3H7nGOVWDJEwKfk2JE3wPJ2~Sw-XCeCEcr7~umpXYeLk6kjxTvHNPwWj7aJEg4breFOA0~W-zf0VaIjNg80NlErkT-QxU3q5kA7V~zvRFikJ0wAE4TAKCliWcAZeUVMgMCVs-ONOxho9OlGVsJ8UYuHw0DXc5T7zQ72vHLZIV-gkyjKKK9hocM51v2wCwCP5oE0fxEIcrYTbbwfsjMNUUW8hTqd37d220K7v8ZSFsxDDOrAQxLbJs0m3JyrcZEk5z9LGNilIwqEhqjMPRw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The MUSE spectrum of the lens galaxy J0403, extracted within the Einstein aperture, i.e. radius 1.5\u00a0arcsec, after masking pixels strongly affected by the arcs. For clarity, only the region of the MUSE spectral range (4750\u20136850\u2009\u00c5) used for fitting the stellar population models is shown. The observed spectrum (red) shows that the galaxy has an absorption-dominated spectrum, but also has a nebular line emission component, seen most easily in the H\u00a0\u03b1\u2013[N\u2009ii] region. The corrected spectrum, shown in grey, after subtracting an emission-line model, fitted to the H\u00a0\u03b1\u2013[N\u2009ii] complex, and assuming Case B recombination and galactic (no internal) extinction, to predict the H\u2009\u03b2 emission. Below, we reproduce the emission-corrected spectrum, and show the best-fitting stellar population (green), derived from the models of Conroy &amp; van Dokkum (2012a). This model has age 12\u2009Gyr and metal abundances typical for massive ellipticals. The fit residuals, shown in black, have a 1\u2009per\u00a0cent rms. In fitting this model, we exclude the H\u2009\u03b1 region, as well as wavelengths contaminated by atmospheric artefacts (indicated by \u2295) and the MW neutral sodium doublet.\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1188fig3.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/117976019/sty1188fig3.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1188fig3.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"117976019\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/2/10.1093_mnras_sty1188/2/sty1188fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611465988&Signature=mklNCBQDirkhBnh2W7LkXp7ko4DIPypFhCxTnHigyW9qT4G8RMbZ~Jm5-Qm84bYH7uGUVc7nSBid71Dpo3NhSsRdEOAqgi9X9sApLCJfIqCjutOtUIm~eRaQOuiKOWBCAWhC2zzBfBAQvoC7SCivIrSA2fQKwod~lTmjFFb-7XynnFTDTHMYPebWe5jvfRND4UmCe9ji019YI6qveORw56e0iHH6lwp0NeDwcth7jX6TnXhX9Z9evnoguplHwolEPSsvC0u0t3hKntiUJCq2B6bdpY3txHrzMqj-E9SSiotvK3v8itKRhKTBSBCiMjHkSychA306C9awmJMOtIPlvQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=117976019&ar=4993538&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1188fig3.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The MUSE spectrum of the lens galaxy J0403, extracted within the Einstein aperture, i.e. radius 1.5\u00a0arcsec, after masking pixels strongly affected by the arcs. For clarity, only the region of the MUSE spectral range (4750\u20136850\u2009\u00c5) used for fitting the stellar population models is shown. The observed spectrum (red) shows that the galaxy has an absorption-dominated spectrum, but also has a nebular line emission component, seen most easily in the H\u00a0\u03b1\u2013[N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] region. The corrected spectrum, shown in grey, after subtracting an emission-line model, fitted to the H\u00a0\u03b1\u2013[N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] complex, and assuming Case B recombination and galactic (no internal) extinction, to predict the H\u2009\u03b2 emission. Below, we reproduce the emission-corrected spectrum, and show the best-fitting stellar population (green), derived from the models of Conroy &amp; van Dokkum (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012a</a>). This model has age 12\u2009Gyr and metal abundances typical for massive ellipticals. The fit residuals, shown in black, have a 1\u2009per\u00a0cent rms. In fitting this model, we exclude the H\u2009\u03b1 region, as well as wavelengths contaminated by atmospheric artefacts (indicated by \u2295) and the MW neutral sodium doublet.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/2/10.1093_mnras_sty1188/2/m_sty1188fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611465988&amp;Signature=S~fJSDrysQMxBUmztFy9zdlNhMHNLw~sSMjm9CUUSYhrPiyMPn-1WKz9pw0cVN82T3rs3H7nGOVWDJEwKfk2JE3wPJ2~Sw-XCeCEcr7~umpXYeLk6kjxTvHNPwWj7aJEg4breFOA0~W-zf0VaIjNg80NlErkT-QxU3q5kA7V~zvRFikJ0wAE4TAKCliWcAZeUVMgMCVs-ONOxho9OlGVsJ8UYuHw0DXc5T7zQ72vHLZIV-gkyjKKK9hocM51v2wCwCP5oE0fxEIcrYTbbwfsjMNUUW8hTqd37d220K7v8ZSFsxDDOrAQxLbJs0m3JyrcZEk5z9LGNilIwqEhqjMPRw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The MUSE spectrum of the lens galaxy J0403, extracted within the Einstein aperture, i.e. radius 1.5\u00a0arcsec, after masking pixels strongly affected by the arcs. For clarity, only the region of the MUSE spectral range (4750\u20136850\u2009\u00c5) used for fitting the stellar population models is shown. The observed spectrum (red) shows that the galaxy has an absorption-dominated spectrum, but also has a nebular line emission component, seen most easily in the H\u00a0\u03b1\u2013[N\u2009ii] region. The corrected spectrum, shown in grey, after subtracting an emission-line model, fitted to the H\u00a0\u03b1\u2013[N\u2009ii] complex, and assuming Case B recombination and galactic (no internal) extinction, to predict the H\u2009\u03b2 emission. Below, we reproduce the emission-corrected spectrum, and show the best-fitting stellar population (green), derived from the models of Conroy &amp; van Dokkum (2012a). This model has age 12\u2009Gyr and metal abundances typical for massive ellipticals. The fit residuals, shown in black, have a 1\u2009per\u00a0cent rms. In fitting this model, we exclude the H\u2009\u03b1 region, as well as wavelengths contaminated by atmospheric artefacts (indicated by \u2295) and the MW neutral sodium doublet.\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1188fig3.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/117976019/sty1188fig3.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1188fig3.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"117976019\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/2/10.1093_mnras_sty1188/2/sty1188fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611465988&Signature=mklNCBQDirkhBnh2W7LkXp7ko4DIPypFhCxTnHigyW9qT4G8RMbZ~Jm5-Qm84bYH7uGUVc7nSBid71Dpo3NhSsRdEOAqgi9X9sApLCJfIqCjutOtUIm~eRaQOuiKOWBCAWhC2zzBfBAQvoC7SCivIrSA2fQKwod~lTmjFFb-7XynnFTDTHMYPebWe5jvfRND4UmCe9ji019YI6qveORw56e0iHH6lwp0NeDwcth7jX6TnXhX9Z9evnoguplHwolEPSsvC0u0t3hKntiUJCq2B6bdpY3txHrzMqj-E9SSiotvK3v8itKRhKTBSBCiMjHkSychA306C9awmJMOtIPlvQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=117976019&ar=4993538&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1188fig3.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The MUSE spectrum of the lens galaxy J0403, extracted within the Einstein aperture, i.e. radius 1.5\u00a0arcsec, after masking pixels strongly affected by the arcs. For clarity, only the region of the MUSE spectral range (4750\u20136850\u2009\u00c5) used for fitting the stellar population models is shown. The observed spectrum (red) shows that the galaxy has an absorption-dominated spectrum, but also has a nebular line emission component, seen most easily in the H\u00a0\u03b1\u2013[N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] region. The corrected spectrum, shown in grey, after subtracting an emission-line model, fitted to the H\u00a0\u03b1\u2013[N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] complex, and assuming Case B recombination and galactic (no internal) extinction, to predict the H\u2009\u03b2 emission. Below, we reproduce the emission-corrected spectrum, and show the best-fitting stellar population (green), derived from the models of Conroy &amp; van Dokkum (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012a</a>). This model has age 12\u2009Gyr and metal abundances typical for massive ellipticals. The fit residuals, shown in black, have a 1\u2009per\u00a0cent rms. In fitting this model, we exclude the H\u2009\u03b1 region, as well as wavelengths contaminated by atmospheric artefacts (indicated by \u2295) and the MW neutral sodium doublet.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Using <em>i</em>-band imaging from the Pan-STARRS (PS1) survey we measure a total magnitude for J0403 of 14.26\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.04\u00a0mag and an effective (half-light) radius of 5.7\u00a0arcsec (i.e. 3.9\u00a0times the size of <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub>). Within <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub> we measure an aperture magnitude of 16.35\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.01\u2009mag. As the PS1 point spread function (\u2009PSF; \u223c1.1\u2009arcsec) is comparable in size to <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub>, a PSF correction is necessary. We determine the correction by fitting a S\u00e9rsic model via <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> (Peng et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib30\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib30\" data-open=\"bib30\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>), with a convolution kernel derived from a set of adjacent stars. The magnitude difference between the S\u00e9rsic model before and after convolution was 0.22\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.02\u00a0mag and hence we adopt a PSF-corrected Einstein-aperture magnitude of <em>i</em><sub>Ein</sub>\u00a0=\u00a016.13\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.02\u00a0mag.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=117976021 id=\"117976021\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4>4 LENSING MASS AND THE IMF</h2>\r\n<div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">Gravitational lensing provides a precise measurement of the total projected mass within the Einstein radius (<em>M</em><sub>Ein</sub>). Measuring <em>M</em><sub>Ein</sub> and the luminosity within the same aperture (<em>L</em><sub>Ein</sub>), the resulting mass-to-light ratio can be related to the IMF mass excess parameter, \u03b1. This factor is defined as <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"\" content-id=\"\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation*}\r\n\\alpha = \\dfrac{\\Upsilon }{\\Upsilon _{\\rm Ref}} = \\dfrac{M^*_{\\rm Ein}}{ L_{\\rm Ein}} \\times \\dfrac{1}{\\Upsilon _{\\rm Ref}} = \\dfrac{M_{\\rm Ein} - M^{\\rm DM}_{\\rm Ein}}{\\Upsilon _{\\rm Ref} L_{\\rm Ein}} \\ \\,,\r\n\\end{equation*} </div></div></div>where <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$M^{\\rm DM}_{\\rm Ein}$|</span> is the dark matter component. We compare \u03d2, the observed stellar mass-to-light ratio, to \u03d2<sub>Ref</sub>, a reference mass-to-light ratio for a modelled stellar population with comparable properties (i.e. metallicity, age), with a fixed Kroupa (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>) IMF. Hence a Kroupa IMF has \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a01 by definition, while a Salpeter IMF (Salpeter <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1955</a>), with more low-mass stars, has \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a01.55.</div><p class=\"chapter-para\">We compute <em>M</em><sub>Ein</sub> using the symmetric lens equation (see Smith et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>, section 4.1). We adopt cosmological parameters from the 7-yr <em>Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe</em> (<em>WMAP</em>), i.e. <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a070.4\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>\u2009Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>, \u03a9<sub>m</sub>=\u00a00.272, and \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.728 (Komatsu et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib21\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib21\" data-open=\"bib21\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>), to calculate the lensing geometry for redshifts in the CMB frame (<em>z</em>=\u00a0<sub>CMB</sub>0.065\u00a069,\u20090.191\u00a030 and <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\dfrac{D_{\\rm l} D_{\\rm s}}{D_{\\rm ls}}$|</span>\u00a0=\u00a0400.5\u2009Mpc). We derive a total projected mass, <em>M</em><sub>Ein</sub>\u00a0=\u00a010.64\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.23\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>10</sup> M<sub>\u2299</sub>, with the 2\u2009per\u00a0cent uncertainty dominated by the measurement of <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub>. Including a small ellipticity (<em>e</em>\u2009\u2248\u20090.1 as measured from <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span>) increases the mass by 3\u2009per\u00a0cent, and the inclusion of a small external shear (\u2009&lt;5/1\u2009per\u00a0cent for SIS/SIE) reproduces the image positions perfectly. In order to account for these additional possible complexities, we revise the uncertainty in <em>M</em><sub>Ein</sub> to \u223c4\u2009per\u00a0cent, i.e. 0.4\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>10</sup>\u2009M<sub>\u2299</sub>.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The DM mass component is estimated following Smith et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>), using the <span class=\"small-caps\">eagle</span> hydrodynamical cosmological simulation (Schaye et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib33\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib33\" data-open=\"bib33\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>). We measure the average DM mass which would be projected inside an aperture of 1.8\u00a0kpc, averaged over all <span class=\"small-caps\">eagle</span> haloes hosting galaxies with stellar velocity dispersions &gt;275\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>. This indicates a contribution of M<span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$_{\\rm Ein}^{\\rm DM}$|</span>\u00a0=\u00a02.01\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.36\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>10</sup>\u2009M<sub>\u2299</sub> (i.e. 19\u2009per\u00a0cent of M<sub>Ein</sub>), which yields an aperture stellar mass <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$M^*_{\\rm Ein}$|</span> of 8.63\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.54\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>10</sup>\u2009M<sub>\u2299</sub>.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Other mass contributions within the Einstein aperture are expected to be small. For typical ETGs from the <span class=\"small-caps\">atlas</span><sup>3<em>D</em></sup> sample the gas contribution measured within 2<em>R</em><sub>eff</sub> is \u223c10<sup>9</sup>\u2009M<sub>\u2299</sub> for the cold gas (Young et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib45\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib45\" data-open=\"bib45\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>, <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib46\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib46\" data-open=\"bib46\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>), and \u223c10<sup>9</sup>\u2009M<sub>\u2299</sub> for the hot gas (Su et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib41\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib41\" data-open=\"bib41\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>). As the Einstein aperture is significantly smaller than this, the gas contribution is expected to be less than 1\u2009per\u00a0cent of the measured lensing mass. The average contribution from a black hole hosted by a comparable (\u03c3\u2009\u2248\u2009300\u00a0kms<sup>\u22121</sup>) galaxy is \u223c2\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>9</sup>\u2009M<sub>\u2299</sub> (van den Bosch <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib44\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib44\" data-open=\"bib44\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>), i.e. \u223c2\u00a0per\u00a0cent of the lensing mass. We do not apply a correction for these effects.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The lens aperture luminosity is calculated from the PSF corrected <em>i</em><sub>Ein</sub>, with additional corrections for the line-of-sight galactic extinction and the <em>k-</em>correction.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Despite being located at fairly high galactic latitude (<em>b</em>\u00a0=\u00a0\u221238\u00b0), J0403 lies in a region of relatively high galactic extinction, with <em>A<sub>i</sub></em>\u00a0=\u00a00.26 according to the Schlafly &amp; Finkbeiner (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib34\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib34\" data-open=\"bib34\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>). Alternatively, maps based on PS1 stellar photometry (Schlafly et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Green et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>) indicate slightly smaller values, with <em>A<sub>i</sub></em>\u00a0=\u00a00.22. The MUSE spectrum shows a clear galactic Na\u2009D absorption doublet (\u223c1\u2009\u00c5 equivalent width), which supports the presence of substantial interstellar material along this sightline. We adopt a correction of 0.24\u2009mag, and allocate an error of 0.04\u2009mag (16\u2009per\u00a0cent of the extinction in magnitudes, following Schlegel, Finkbeiner &amp; Davis (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib36\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib36\" data-open=\"bib36\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1998</a>). The <em>k</em>-correction is estimated from the lens <em>g</em>\u2013<em>i</em> colour index (1.7) to be <em>k<sub>i</sub></em>\u00a0=\u00a00.04\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.01\u2009mag (Chilingarian, Melchior &amp; Zolotukhin <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Chilingarian &amp; Zolotukhin <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The final corrected Einstein-aperture apparent magnitude is <em>i</em><sub>Ein</sub>\u00a0=\u00a015.87\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.05, which for the adopted cosmology (<em>D</em><sub>L</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0295.7\u2009Mpc), and the solar AB <em>i</em>-band absolute magnitude of 4.534 (Blanton &amp; Roweis <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>), yields an aperture luminosity of 2.59\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.12 \u00d7 10<sup>10</sup>\u2009L<sub>\u2299</sub>. The uncertainty is dominated by the applied corrections. Combined with <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$M^*_{\\rm Ein}$|</span> the observed stellar mass-to-light ratio is \u03d2\u00a0=\u00a03.33\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.26 solar units.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">To convert from mass-to-light ratio (\u03d2) to the IMF mass factor \u03b1, we need an estimate for the reference mass-to-light ratio for a given fiducial IMF (here Kroupa <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>), which depends on the stellar population properties: age (or star formation history), metallicity, etc. To estimate these parameters, we make use of the MUSE spectrum of the lens galaxy extracted within the Einstein aperture, shown in Fig.<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a> (red line). However, the presence of significant nebular emission, combined with limited spectral coverage in the blue, presents some challenges. Specifically, the stellar population age is constrained only by the H\u2009\u03b1 and H\u2009\u03b2 absorption lines, but both are contaminated by gas emission, to different extent.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We tackle the nebular emission by first fitting a three-Gaussian model to the H\u2009\u03b1 and [N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] lines, with an assumed stellar continuum model. The relative amplitudes of the [N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] lines are fixed, and all lines are assumed to have the same velocity and width. The amplitudes of H\u2009\u03b1 and the [N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] doublet are allowed to vary. From this model, we can predict the H\u2009\u03b2 emission line, assuming Case B recombination for the intrinsic line ratio, and a relative attenuation factor of 1.135 for H\u2009\u03b2 from the MW extinction (we assume no internal extinction for this exercise). Because the underlying H\u2009\u03b1 absorption has only low sensitivity to the stellar population age, the emission correction is quite robust against changes to the age assumed in the first step.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">After making this correction to remove the emission-infilling of the spectrum at H\u2009\u03b2, we perform a full-spectrum fit over the interval 4500\u20136400\u2009\u00c5, using single-burst models from Conroy &amp; van Dokkum (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012b</a>), (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>, green). We allow for variation in abundances of Mg, Fe, Na, and C, as well as variation in age. Formally, the fit implies an old population, with age 12.0\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.0\u2009Gyr, high metallicity [Z/H]\u2009\u2248\u2009[Mg/H]\u2009\u2248\u2009+0.15\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.02 and typical massive elliptical galaxy abundance ratios [Mg/Fe]\u2009\u2248\u2009+0.3, [Na/Fe]\u2009\u2248\u2009+0.5, and [C/Fe]\u2009\u2248\u2009+0.2.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The reference mass-to-light ratio is estimated with Conroy, Gunn &amp; White (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>) stellar population models accessed via <span class=\"small-caps\">ezgal</span> (Mancone &amp; Gonzalez <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib24\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib24\" data-open=\"bib24\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>). For a Kroupa (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>) IMF, with an old, solar metallicity stellar population (<em>z</em><sub>form</sub>\u00a0=\u00a03), the models predict <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\Upsilon ^{\\rm *}_{\\rm Ref}$|</span>\u00a0=\u00a02.90\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.10. The uncertainty is derived from small variations in the metallicity and age (e.g. <em>z</em><sub>form</sub>=\u00a02.5\u20133.5, metallicity 1\u20131.5 solar). The resulting IMF mass excess parameter, with its statistical error, is \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a01.15\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.10.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Several adopted parameters and assumptions affect the value of \u03b1 derived above. If we had prescribed the Planck Collaboration XIII (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib31\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib31\" data-open=\"bib31\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) cosmology, \u03b1 would be 4\u2009per\u00a0cent lower, due to the variation in <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>. If we had attributed the total lensing mass entirely to the stellar component (i.e. no DM inside <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub>), we would have found \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a01.42, a 25\u2009per\u00a0cent increase. If we had assumed, due to the limited blue (most age sensitive) coverage from MUSE, that the population may be 8.5\u2009Gyr old (<em>z</em><sub>form</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01.5), \u03b1 would have increased by 14\u2009per\u00a0cent. If we had adopted the Maraston (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>) models, \u03b1 would increase by 10\u00a0per\u00a0cent.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=117976034 id=\"117976034\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec5>5 DISCUSSION AND CONCLUSIONS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We have discovered a new low-redshift gravitational lens, with a bright background galaxy, and a small Einstein radius, probing the stellar-dominated core of a massive elliptical galaxy. We have measured the lensing mass and the <em>i</em>-band Einstein aperture luminosity to estimate the IMF mismatch parameter, \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a01.15\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.10. We attribute a further uncertainty of (\u223c15\u00a0per\u00a0cent) \u00b1\u00a00.17 for the systematics. This measurement hence favours a lightweight (MW-like) IMF, rather than a heavy (e.g. Salpeter) one.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In comparison to the SNELLS systems, J0403 is more distant (<em>z</em><sub>lens</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.066 versus 0.031\u20130.052), but the background source is at much lower redshift (<em>z</em><sub>src</sub>=\u00a00.19 versus 0.93\u20132.14). As a result, the Einstein radius is smaller in angular terms (1.5\u00a0arcsec versus 2.2\u20132.9\u00a0arcsec). Thus despite the greater lens distance, <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub> projects to a similar radius in physical units (1.8\u00a0kpc versus 1.5\u20132.2\u00a0kpc) or in galaxy-scale units (0.25<em>R</em><sub>eff</sub> versus 0.3\u20130.7<em>\u00a0R</em><sub>eff</sub>). Like the SNELLS galaxies, J0403 has high velocity dispersion and metal abundances typical for massive elliptical galaxies. Furthermore, the lensing aperture mass is compatible with the SNELLS results, favouring an MW-like IMF, and inconsistent with very bottom-heavy IMFs.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Combining our estimate of \u03b1 for J0403 with the three estimates from SNELLS (taking the values from Newman et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>; Collier et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>, i.e. SNL-0, SNL-1, SNL-2\u00a0=\u00a01.05\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.09, 1.17\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.09, 0.96\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.10), as shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>, we can infer limits on the <em>intrinsic</em> distribution of this quantity among \u03c3\u2009\u2248\u2009300\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup> ETGs. We compute the joint likelihood of the four \u03b1 measurements as a function of the unknown population mean \u27e8\u03b1\u27e9 and dispersion \u03c3<sub>int</sub>, accounting for the measurement errors. Marginalizing over \u03c3<sub>int</sub>, with a flat prior, we infer \u27e8\u03b1\u27e9\u00a0=\u00a01.09\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.08 (the error is larger than for a calculation which assumes no intrinsic dispersion). Marginalizing over the mean, we infer only an upper limit on the scatter, with \u03c3<sub>int</sub>\u00a0\u2009&lt;\u00a0\u20090.32 at 90\u00a0per\u00a0cent confidence. (For comparison the same treatment applied to SNELLS alone yields an upper limit of \u03c3<sub>int</sub>\u00a0\u2009&lt;\u00a0\u20090.7, highlighting the impact of adding just one new measurement to the analysis.)</p>                        <a id=\"117976038\" scrollto-destination=\"117976038\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig4\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 4.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/2/10.1093_mnras_sty1188/2/m_sty1188fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611465988&amp;Signature=QYBxsBxr34mqwH9Vai5p2dBypXOo18M9Atu8Y0EZHRneVC3KKuaA5GohU4JKSolpoDq4qi-hST4Gx91S1k3CrpPPYrLv3jLO7IdNNgqq5naZA7kVxfHdZyvcz2p4khUoojPl8GcfjvJV9H2yiSxCckx53JWwLa7qBmHUd6VL2Mex4-3nkVBxW7IHBPt-islyzrawyaZqmbRAAKrJrODFDap1xfgV4Q5rY7l7L4CZRBM~fuMbZhXm5fnRAdrQ9g~9tVFUD5KPvu2bMaKZUd47PKYpl0~JB1su6vIsgsNSJdJlvlqKqz1Ile80Qvxi1Y34VJva-Qb-0mZLTkPRJYGcNg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The distribution of the mass excess parameter (\u03b1) for the combined SNELLS and J0403 sample. In blue, is the SNELLS sample with results for SNL-0 from Newman et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), and SNL-1, SNL-2 from Collier et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>). The result for J0403 from this paper is marked in red. The sample average is \u27e8\u03b1\u27e9\u00a0=\u00a01.09\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.08, with an inferred <em>intrinsic</em> scatter of &lt;\u20090.32, at 90\u00a0per\u00a0cent confidence. These galaxies on average favour an MW-like IMF in preference to a Salpeter or heavier IMF.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">One important difference between J0403 and the SNELLS sample, which is relevant for future work, is that the J0403 arcs are both very bright and appear to be quite extended. Hence among all of the known low-redshift lenses, this system is uniquely suitable for pixelized lens inversion methods, which can yield much more powerful constraints on the mass distribution (e.g. Nightingale, Dye &amp; Massey <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib27\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib27\" data-open=\"bib27\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>; Oldham et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib29\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib29\" data-open=\"bib29\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). Upcoming observations with <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> will provide high-resolution images for use in such studies.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">J0403 is by far the brightest lens system so far identified from our MUSE archival study. The results from the full sample, which is sensitive to much fainter background sources, will be the subject of a future paper (Collier et\u00a0al. in preparation). We have also begun a programme to acquire targeted MUSE observations for selected high-likelihood lenses. The discovery of the J0403 system bodes well for the success of such searches. Together with efforts elsewhere (e.g. Talbot et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib42\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib42\" data-open=\"bib42\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>), we anticipate exploiting meaningful samples of lenses at <em>z</em> \u2009\u2272\u2009 0.1 in the near future, to address the continuing puzzle of IMF variations in massive elliptical galaxies.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=117976041 id=\"117976041\" class=\"backacknowledgements-title\" >ACKNOWLEDGEMENTS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">WPC was supported by an STFC studentship (ST/N50404X/1). widget-instance-OUP_Article_FullText_Widget\">\n        <div class=\"module-widget\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget-items\" data-widgetname=\"ArticleFulltext\">\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                    <h2 scrollto-destination=117975996 id=\"117975996\" class=\"abstract-title\" >ABSTRACT</h2>\r\n<section class=\"abstract\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">We report the discovery of a new low-redshift galaxy-scale gravitational lens, identified from a systematic search of publicly available MUSE observations. The lens galaxy, 2MASXJ04035024\u22120239275, is a giant elliptical at <em>z</em>=\u00a00.066\u00a004 with a velocity dispersion of \u03c3\u00a0=\u00a0314\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>. The lensed source has a redshift of 0.191\u00a065 and forms a pair of bright images on either side of the lens centre. The Einstein radius is 1.5\u2009arcsec, projecting to 1.8\u00a0kpc, which is just one quarter of the galaxy effective radius. After correcting for an estimated 19\u2009per\u00a0cent dark matter contribution, we find that the stellar mass-to-light ratio from lensing is consistent with that expected for a Milky Way initial mass function (IMF). Combining the new system with three previously studied low-redshift lenses of similar \u03c3, the derived mean mass excess factor (relative to a Kroupa IMF) is \u27e8\u03b1\u27e9\u00a0=\u00a01.09\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.08. With all four systems, the intrinsic scatter in \u03b1 for massive elliptical galaxies can be limited to &lt;0.32, at 90\u2009per\u00a0cent confidence.</p></section>                        <div class=\"article-metadata-panel clearfix\"></div>\r\n<div class=\"kwd-group\"><a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;gravitational lensing: strong&quot;\">gravitational lensing: strong</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: elliptical and lenticular, cD&quot;\">galaxies: elliptical and lenticular, cD</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: stellar content&quot;\">galaxies: stellar content</a></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=117975998 id=\"117975998\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec1>1 INTRODUCTION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The initial mass function (IMF) is fundamental to understanding galaxy formation and evolution, as well as to interpreting observed properties (e.g. estimating stellar masses). Within the different star-forming environments in the Milky Way (MW), the stellar IMF is well constrained, and approximately invariant (Bastian, Covey &amp; Meyer <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib2\" data-open=\"bib2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Offner et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>). Deviations towards a flatter IMF have been reported for some resolved ultrafaint Local Group dwarfs (Geha et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">For galaxies beyond the Local Group, resolved studies are not possible, and the IMF must be inferred from the integrated light and/or gravitational mass tracers. Broadly the observational techniques fall into two categories. The first method infers the stellar population via high signal-to-noise spectroscopy. The strength of gravity sensitive absorption lines is measured by fitting detailed stellar population synthesis templates. The second method indirectly measures the stellar population by comparing a stellar mass-to-light ratio (<em>M</em>/<em>L</em>) measured from stellar dynamics or strong lensing, to a reference <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> from a fixed IMF stellar population model. Studies independently utilizing both techniques have found evidence for an increasingly \u2018heavy\u2019 IMF (more measured mass than a fixed IMF model predicts) in the most massive Early Type Galaxiess (ETGs) (e.g. Treu et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib43\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib43\" data-open=\"bib43\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Cappellari et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>; Conroy &amp; van Dokkum <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012b</a>; La Barbera et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The mass measurements from gravitational lensing require careful treatment to disentangle the contributions of stellar mass and dark matter (DM). For example, Treu et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib43\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib43\" data-open=\"bib43\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) analysed lenses at <em>z</em> \u223c \u20090.2 by combining lensing and stellar kinematics to constrain the parameters of a two-component mass model. This approach involves several assumptions (spherical geometry, constant stellar mass-to-light ratio, etc.). In contrast, <em>nearby</em> (<em>z</em> \u2009\u2264 \u20090.1) lenses offer a geometry in which the Einstein radius (<em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub>) is reached at smaller physical radii and hence probes the dense stellar-dominated core. In such cases the relative uncertainty from DM is minimized, as the ratio of dark to stellar matter is reduced and \u2018pure\u2019 lensing constraints on the stellar mass can be obtained (i.e. with no additional information from stellar kinematics).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">At present, the best studied <em>z</em>\u2009 &lt; \u20090.1 lensing ellipticals are those discovered from the SINFONI Nearby Elliptical Lens Locator Survey (SNELLS) (Smith, Lucey &amp; Conroy <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>). This targeted approach used an integral field unit (IFU) to search for background emitters behind massive elliptical galaxies, finding SNL-1 (<em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.031) and SNL-2 (<em>z</em>=\u00a00.052), and \u2018rediscovering\u2019 the previously known lens SNL-0 (<em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.034) (Smith et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib39\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib39\" data-open=\"bib39\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>; Smith &amp; Lucey <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib38\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib38\" data-open=\"bib38\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>). The SNELLS galaxies yielded lensing masses in strong disagreement with \u2018heavy\u2019 IMFs for massive ETGs, instead measuring <em>M</em>/<em>L</em> consistent with an MW-like IMF (Kroupa <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>), both in low-resolution ground-based (Smith et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>), and in high-resolution space-based observations (Collier, Smith &amp; Lucey <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">With only three galaxies, the possibility that the SNELLS sample <em>by chance</em> was drawn from the \u2018tail\u2019 of an intrinsically broad distribution in IMFs cannot be ruled out. As such, with low-number statistics, increasing the sample size is essential to investigate further this conclusion, and to test whether these lenses are representative of the parent population.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Discovering low-redshift lenses is challenging due to the high surface brightness of the massive foreground galaxy. While SNELLS pre-selected high-probability lenses using velocity dispersion measurements, an alternative approach exploiting large multi-IFU surveys like SAMI and MaNGA (Bryant et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>; Bundy et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib5\" data-open=\"bib5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>) has recently yielded new systems (Smith <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib37\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib37\" data-open=\"bib37\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>; Talbot et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib42\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib42\" data-open=\"bib42\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>). A third technique, which we are currently pursuing, is to search for lensed line emitters behind galaxies targeted for other science goals, using data from public archives. Observations made with MUSE (Multi-Unit Spectroscopic Explorer) (Bacon et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>) on the European Southern Observatory (ESO) Very Large Telescope are well suited to this method, due to the very high sensitivity and wide spectral range (4350\u20139300\u2009\u00c5) of the instrument.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this paper, we present the discovery of the first multiply imaged strong gravitational lens from this programme. In Section <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec2\"></span><a href=\"#sec2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">2</a>, we briefly describe the modified lens search process. In Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec3\"></span><a href=\"#sec3\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">3</a> we present the new lens system properties. In Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec4\"></span><a href=\"#sec4\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">4</a> we present lensing mass constraints and the IMF mass excess parameter, and then compare, and combine our results with the SNELLS sample in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec5\"></span><a href=\"#sec5\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">5</a>.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=117976006 id=\"117976006\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2>2 A MUSE ARCHIVAL LENS SEARCH</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Our new lensing system was identified in the course of a systematic search for multiply imaged line emitters behind low-redshift ETGs in public archival MUSE data. A full description of this programme will be presented elsewhere (Collier et\u00a0al., in preparation); here, we briefly summarize the sample selection and main processing steps, to provide context for the new discovery.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">For this lens search, we are analysing public MUSE observations which overlap with luminous nearby galaxies selected from the 2MASS Redshift Survey (Huchra et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib19\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib19\" data-open=\"bib19\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>). We focus our search on targets with redshift 5000\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>\u2009&lt;\u2009<em>cz</em>\u2009&lt;\u200920\u2009000\u2009 km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>, and <em>M<sub>K</sub></em>\u2009&lt;\u2009\u221225.7.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The analysis begins with \u2018Phase 3\u2019 pipeline-reduced products retrieved from the ESO Science Archive. Our primary goal is to detect faint lensed background emitters behind the central regions of nearby ETGs. Before attempting line detection, we therefore process the pipeline-reduced data cubes to subtract the bright stellar foreground, effectively fitting elliptical isophote models to each wavelength channel. The residual cubes are noise-normalized and filtered to suppress instrumental artefacts.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Candidate emission lines are then identified above a significance threshold in the processed residual cubes. Spectra for likely sources are extracted, and redshifts are estimated automatically using <span class=\"small-caps\">marz</span> (Hinton et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>). Finally, the resulting object catalogue is visually inspected, and searched for any spatially separated line emitters with similar redshifts, which are potentially multiply imaged background galaxies.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Although we optimize our search method to detect faint background emission, the first galaxy-scale lens discovered was over 100\u00a0times brighter than our detection limit. Hence we report this system as a \u2018special case\u2019 in this paper.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=117976012 id=\"117976012\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3>3 THE NEW LENS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">2MASXJ04035024\u22120239275 (hereafter J0403) is a luminous (<em>M<sub>K</sub></em>\u00a0=\u00a0\u221225.7) elliptical galaxy at heliocentric redshift <em>z</em><sub>H</sub>=\u00a00.066\u00a055 (Jones et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib20\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib20\" data-open=\"bib20\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>). In imaging from the Pan-STARRS PS1 survey (Chambers et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) J0403 shows a smooth inner light profile, well fitted by a S\u00e9rsic profile with index <em>n</em>\u2009\u00a0\u2248\u00a0\u20094. In the outer regions (radius &gt;\u2009 20\u2009arcsec), some low-surface-brightness (<em>i</em>band \u223c25\u2009mag arcsec<sup>\u22122</sup>) tidal structures are visible. J0403 is a relatively isolated galaxy, with no overdensity of similar-colour objects visible in PS1 imaging. The closest comparable brightness companion (2MASXJ04034531\u22120236595) is a spiral galaxy, fainter by 1.3 mag, with relative velocity +550\u2009km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>, at a projected distance of \u223c210\u00a0kpc (2.7\u00a0arcmin). There are no catalogued clusters with comparable redshift within a radius of 2\u2009\u00a0deg (9.5\u2009\u00a0Mpc).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">J0403 was observed with MUSE as part of a study targeting supernova hosts [ESO programme 098.D-0115(A); PI L. Galbany]. The exposure time was \u223c2400\u2009s, with seeing \u22720.8\u00a0arcsec (estimated full width at half-maximum from compact sources). The MUSE field of view is 1\u00a0\u00d7\u00a01\u00a0arcmin<sup>2</sup> (\u223c80\u00a0\u00d7\u00a080\u00a0kpc<sup>2</sup>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In the MUSE data (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1a</a>), the light profile of J0403 is smooth at small radii, with evidence for the faint tidal features seen in the PS1 imaging. Extracting narrow-band images (Fig.<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1b</a>) highlights a pair of extended background emitters (arcs A and B) either side of the galaxy centre. Strong H\u2009\u03b1, [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>], and H\u2009\u03b2 lines are present in the spectra of both emitters, with the same redshift, <em>z</em><sub>H</sub>=\u00a00.191\u00a065 (Fig.<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a>). The respective velocity offset between A and B is \u226450\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>, providing strong evidence of a common source. When the continuum is subtracted, image A shows slight curvature at low-surface-brightness levels (Fig.<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1c</a>). There is some evidence of a small velocity gradient (\u223c50\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup> peak-to-peak) across arc A, which is mirrored in arc B. A broad-band counterpart to arc A is readily visible in the PS1 imaging, and the counter-arc is recovered after subtraction of a smooth light profile for J0403. The balance of evidence in this case strongly favours a lensing interpretation.<sup><span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn1\" data-open=\"fn1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\">1</a></sup></p>                        <a id=\"117976016\" scrollto-destination=\"117976016\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig1\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 1.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/2/10.1093_mnras_sty1188/2/m_sty1188fig1.jpeg?Expires=1611465988&amp;Signature=Hp1FI9BjKrhNHdJDajrtU~VAmLN99fMKeez3oY~NrRDwdMIyWm34oimbBs9eeBsSVItdsGG68lfx7ZomHeHVT54llapFoFlnlcbxaTqHE8cksBbWkvvKvTZ8hUJReNhekz5uCqSal6Os8D~yvl~Tcx8wEmQLIFHtXkIPparBDC8zxkHrzDa-JZ1mSTIr82SHvltObPaaGvmhkKm7P6F2mUAouUcWBYM6rJec02kq8rzdBMHAi4K882ga5USk0-rEk8atofs-feAZaePXNLfr8A3K9CSqf~EG3k4OFZEfGVsUR3cyqC5aFnrr9tdrNT7XQ6WPkUO5bdAe2hGjtkoAgw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"MUSE data of J0403, within different wavelength ranges (shown in the top right). In (a) we display the arc spectrum extracted from a continuum-subtracted residual data cube, showing the bright H\u00a0\u03b1, [N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>], [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>], H\u00a0\u03b2, H\u00a0\u03b3, and [<span class=\"small-caps\">Sii</span>] emission lines, at a redshift of 0.191\u00a065. In (b) and (c), we overlay the emission from images A and B, for the <span class=\"small-caps\">[Oiii]</span> and H\u2009\u03b1 regions, respectively. There is negligible velocity offset (\u227250\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>) between the two spectra, and image A has been subject to a greater magnification.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig2\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 2.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/2/10.1093_mnras_sty1188/2/m_sty1188fig2.jpeg?Expires=1611465988&amp;Signature=zPJSL8qCB8uKO2-nFzcZdLunnk~tY44ckHNbb83XZMKWyMhgTHtuT1Z4fcaC~XoBxW0hZ7vMkP5B3A7Ped5x9T~IwfY14qAAcxdUZ3mLnUY85dJASj67QJVjH~5djO2G3X5xmjwejDvzQirnE2NCeFIpeD3xG7DZ2-lh0X2wu-1nL-Pbc3sRtyuzn4QnPPcOd8rCKGMwzFFSG1sutnk6IS8vgQ1QYB93uDZdWqNTEKooRLnJYHPWacOJArJZ6-D6pHDO6CrmCIzIqVYsDhUNSns58XfAei~aS~4H-BFplDW5nPlcIxDbkmVaF~MVl8CUO7t82ycain~jQlhSZEK~-Q__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"MUSE spectra of the lensed images, with flux in units of 10\u221216\u00a0erg\u2009s\u22121\u00a0cm\u22122\u2009\u00a0\u00c5\u22121. In (a) we display the arc spectrum extracted from a continuum-subtracted residual data cube, showing the bright H\u00a0\u03b1, [N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>], [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>], H\u00a0\u03b2, H\u00a0\u03b3, and [<span class=\"small-caps\">Sii</span>] emission lines, at a redshift of 0.191\u00a065. There is negligible velocity offset (\u227250\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>) between the two spectra, and image A has been subject to a greater magnification.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">The lensed images are separated by 2.94\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.06\u00a0arcsec (\u223c3.75\u2009kpc), where the uncertainty is derived from measurements at various emission lines. For our lensing analysis we adopt <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub> as half of the image separation, 1.47\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.03\u00a0arcsec, and extract an aperture spectrum (\u2264<em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub>) of J0403, shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>. The spectrum displays the strong absorption features typical of an old stellar population, with strong Na\u2009D, Mg\u00a0<span class=\"small-caps\">i</span>, and TiO, along with weak H\u00a0\u03b1 and H\u00a0\u03b2 emission. The measured heliocentric redshift, <em>z</em><sub>H</sub>=\u00a00.066\u00a004, and velocity dispersion of \u03c3\u00a0=\u00a0314\u00a0\u00b1\u00a05\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup> are found by fitting this central region using <span class=\"small-caps\">ppxf</span> (Cappellari &amp; Emsellem <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2004</a>).</p>                        <a id=\"117976019\" scrollto-destination=\"117976019\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/2/10.1093_mnras_sty1188/2/m_sty1188fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611465988&amp;Signature=S~fJSDrysQMxBUmztFy9zdlNhMHNLw~sSMjm9CUUSYhrPiyMPn-1WKz9pw0cVN82T3rs3H7nGOVWDJEwKfk2JE3wPJ2~Sw-XCeCEcr7~umpXYeLk6kjxTvHNPwWj7aJEg4breFOA0~W-zf0VaIjNg80NlErkT-QxU3q5kA7V~zvRFikJ0wAE4TAKCliWcAZeUVMgMCVs-ONOxho9OlGVsJ8UYuHw0DXc5T7zQ72vHLZIV-gkyjKKK9hocM51v2wCwCP5oE0fxEIcrYTbbwfsjMNUUW8hTqd37d220K7v8ZSFsxDDOrAQxLbJs0m3JyrcZEk5z9LGNilIwqEhqjMPRw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The MUSE spectrum of the lens galaxy J0403, extracted within the Einstein aperture, i.e. radius 1.5\u00a0arcsec, after masking pixels strongly affected by the arcs. For clarity, only the region of the MUSE spectral range (4750\u20136850\u2009\u00c5) used for fitting the stellar population models is shown. The observed spectrum (red) shows that the galaxy has an absorption-dominated spectrum, but also has a nebular line emission component, seen most easily in the H\u00a0\u03b1\u2013[N\u2009ii] region. For clarity, only the region of the MUSE spectral range (4750\u20136850\u2009\u00c5) used for fitting the stellar population models is shown. The observed spectrum (red) shows that the galaxy has an absorption-dominated spectrum, but also has a nebular line emission component, seen most easily in the H\u00a0\u03b1\u2013[N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] region. The corrected spectrum, shown in grey, after subtracting an emission-line model, fitted to the H\u00a0\u03b1\u2013[N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] complex, and assuming Case B recombination and galactic (no internal) extinction, to predict the H\u2009\u03b2 emission. Below, we reproduce the emission-corrected spectrum, and show the best-fitting stellar population (green), derived from the models of Conroy &amp; van Dokkum (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012a</a>). This model has age 12\u2009Gyr and metal abundances typical for massive ellipticals. The fit residuals, shown in black, have a 1\u2009per\u00a0cent rms. In fitting this model, we exclude the H\u2009\u03b1 region, as well as wavelengths contaminated by atmospheric artefacts (indicated by \u2295) and the MW neutral sodium doublet.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/2/10.1093_mnras_sty1188/2/m_sty1188fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611465988&amp;Signature=S~fJSDrysQMxBUmztFy9zdlNhMHNLw~sSMjm9CUUSYhrPiyMPn-1WKz9pw0cVN82T3rs3H7nGOVWDJEwKfk2JE3wPJ2~Sw-XCeCEcr7~umpXYeLk6kjxTvHNPwWj7aJEg4breFOA0~W-zf0VaIjNg80NlErkT-QxU3q5kA7V~zvRFikJ0wAE4TAKCliWcAZeUVMgMCVs-ONOxho9OlGVsJ8UYuHw0DXc5T7zQ72vHLZIV-gkyjKKK9hocM51v2wCwCP5oE0fxEIcrYTbbwfsjMNUUW8hTqd37d220K7v8ZSFsxDDOrAQxLbJs0m3JyrcZEk5z9LGNilIwqEhqjMPRw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The MUSE spectrum of the lens galaxy J0403, extracted within the Einstein aperture, i.e. radius 1.5\u00a0arcsec, after masking pixels strongly affected by the arcs.
