In fitting this model, we exclude the H\u2009\u03b1 region, as well as wavelengths contaminated by atmospheric artefacts (indicated by \u2295) and the MW neutral sodium doublet.\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1188fig3.jpg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/117976019/sty1188fig3.jpg\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1188fig3.jpg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"117976019\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/2/10.1093_mnras_sty1188/2/sty1188fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611465988&Signature=mklNCBQDirkhBnh2W7LkXp7ko4DIPypFhCxTnHigyW9qT4G8RMbZ~Jm5-Qm84bYH7uGUVc7nSBid71Dpo3NhSsRdEOAqgi9X9sApLCJfIqCjutOtUIm~eRaQOuiKOWBCAWhC2zzBfBAQvoC7SCivIrSA2fQKwod~lTmjFFb-7XynnFTDTHMYPebWe5jvfRND4UmCe9ji019YI6qveORw56e0iHH6lwp0NeDwcth7jX6TnXhX9Z9evnoguplHwolEPSsvC0u0t3hKntiUJCq2B6bdpY3txHrzMqj-E9SSiotvK3v8itKRhKTBSBCiMjHkSychA306C9awmJMOtIPlvQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=117976019&ar=4993538&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"sty1188fig3.jpg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The MUSE spectrum of the lens galaxy J0403, extracted within the Einstein aperture, i.e. radius 1.5\u00a0arcsec, after masking pixels strongly affected by the arcs. For clarity, only the region of the MUSE spectral range (4750\u20136850\u2009\u00c5) used for fitting the stellar population models is shown. The observed spectrum (red) shows that the galaxy has an absorption-dominated spectrum, but also has a nebular line emission component, seen most easily in the H\u00a0\u03b1\u2013[N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] region. The corrected spectrum, shown in grey, after subtracting an emission-line model, fitted to the H\u00a0\u03b1\u2013[N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] complex, and assuming Case B recombination and galactic (no internal) extinction, to predict the H\u2009\u03b2 emission. Below, we reproduce the emission-corrected spectrum, and show the best-fitting stellar population (green), derived from the models of Conroy &amp; van Dokkum (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012a</a>). This model has age 12\u2009Gyr and metal abundances typical for massive ellipticals. The fit residuals, shown in black, have a 1\u2009per\u00a0cent rms. In fitting this model, we exclude the H\u2009\u03b1 region, as well as wavelengths contaminated by atmospheric artefacts (indicated by \u2295) and the MW neutral sodium doublet.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Using <em>i</em>-band imaging from the Pan-STARRS (PS1) survey we measure a total magnitude for J0403 of 14.26\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.04\u00a0mag and an effective (half-light) radius of 5.7\u00a0arcsec (i.e. 3.9\u00a0times the size of <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub>). Within <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub> we measure an aperture magnitude of 16.35\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.01\u2009mag. As the PS1 point spread function (\u2009PSF; \u223c1.1\u2009arcsec) is comparable in size to <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub>, a PSF correction is necessary. We determine the correction by fitting a S\u00e9rsic model via <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> (Peng et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib30\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib30\" data-open=\"bib30\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>), with a convolution kernel derived from a set of adjacent stars. The magnitude difference between the S\u00e9rsic model before and after convolution was 0.22\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.02\u00a0mag and hence we adopt a PSF-corrected Einstein-aperture magnitude of <em>i</em><sub>Ein</sub>\u00a0=\u00a016.13\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.02\u00a0mag.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=117976021 id=\"117976021\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4>4 LENSING MASS AND THE IMF</h2>\r\n<div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">Gravitational lensing provides a precise measurement of the total projected mass within the Einstein radius (<em>M</em><sub>Ein</sub>). Measuring <em>M</em><sub>Ein</sub> and the luminosity within the same aperture (<em>L</em><sub>Ein</sub>), the resulting mass-to-light ratio can be related to the IMF mass excess parameter, \u03b1. This factor is defined as <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"\" content-id=\"\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation*}\r\n\\alpha = \\dfrac{\\Upsilon }{\\Upsilon _{\\rm Ref}} = \\dfrac{M^*_{\\rm Ein}}{ L_{\\rm Ein}} \\times \\dfrac{1}{\\Upsilon _{\\rm Ref}} = \\dfrac{M_{\\rm Ein} - M^{\\rm DM}_{\\rm Ein}}{\\Upsilon _{\\rm Ref} L_{\\rm Ein}} \\ \\,,\r\n\\end{equation*} </div></div></div>where <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$M^{\\rm DM}_{\\rm Ein}$|</span> is the dark matter component. We compare \u03d2, the observed stellar mass-to-light ratio, to \u03d2<sub>Ref</sub>, a reference mass-to-light ratio for a modelled stellar population with comparable properties (i.e. metallicity, age), with a fixed Kroupa (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>) IMF. Hence a Kroupa IMF has \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a01 by definition, while a Salpeter IMF (Salpeter <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1955</a>), with more low-mass stars, has \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a01.55.</div><p class=\"chapter-para\">We compute <em>M</em><sub>Ein</sub> using the symmetric lens equation (see Smith et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>, section 4.1). We adopt cosmological parameters from the 7-yr <em>Wilkinson Microwave Anisotropy Probe</em> (<em>WMAP</em>), i.e. <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a070.4\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>\u2009Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>, \u03a9<sub>m</sub>=\u00a00.272, and \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.728 (Komatsu et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib21\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib21\" data-open=\"bib21\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>), to calculate the lensing geometry for redshifts in the CMB frame (<em>z</em>=\u00a0<sub>CMB</sub>0.065\u00a069,\u20090.191\u00a030 and <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\dfrac{D_{\\rm l} D_{\\rm s}}{D_{\\rm ls}}$|</span>\u00a0=\u00a0400.5\u2009Mpc). We derive a total projected mass, <em>M</em><sub>Ein</sub>\u00a0=\u00a010.64\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.23\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>10</sup> M<sub>\u2299</sub>, with the 2\u2009per\u00a0cent uncertainty dominated by the measurement of <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub>. Including a small ellipticity (<em>e</em>\u2009\u2248\u20090.1 as measured from <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span>) increases the mass by 3\u2009per\u00a0cent, and the inclusion of a small external shear (\u2009&lt;5/1\u2009per\u00a0cent for SIS/SIE) reproduces the image positions perfectly. In order to account for these additional possible complexities, we revise the uncertainty in <em>M</em><sub>Ein</sub> to \u223c4\u2009per\u00a0cent, i.e. 0.4\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>10</sup>\u2009M<sub>\u2299</sub>.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The DM mass component is estimated following Smith et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>), using the <span class=\"small-caps\">eagle</span> hydrodynamical cosmological simulation (Schaye et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib33\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib33\" data-open=\"bib33\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>). We measure the average DM mass which would be projected inside an aperture of 1.8\u00a0kpc, averaged over all <span class=\"small-caps\">eagle</span> haloes hosting galaxies with stellar velocity dispersions &gt;275\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>. This indicates a contribution of M<span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$_{\\rm Ein}^{\\rm DM}$|</span>\u00a0=\u00a02.01\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.36\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>10</sup>\u2009M<sub>\u2299</sub> (i.e. 19\u2009per\u00a0cent of M<sub>Ein</sub>), which yields an aperture stellar mass <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$M^*_{\\rm Ein}$|</span> of 8.63\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.54\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>10</sup>\u2009M<sub>\u2299</sub>.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Other mass contributions within the Einstein aperture are expected to be small. For typical ETGs from the <span class=\"small-caps\">atlas</span><sup>3<em>D</em></sup> sample the gas contribution measured within 2<em>R</em><sub>eff</sub> is \u223c10<sup>9</sup>\u2009M<sub>\u2299</sub> for the cold gas (Young et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib45\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib45\" data-open=\"bib45\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>, <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib46\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib46\" data-open=\"bib46\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>), and \u223c10<sup>9</sup>\u2009M<sub>\u2299</sub> for the hot gas (Su et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib41\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib41\" data-open=\"bib41\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>). As the Einstein aperture is significantly smaller than this, the gas contribution is expected to be less than 1\u2009per\u00a0cent of the measured lensing mass. The average contribution from a black hole hosted by a comparable (\u03c3\u2009\u2248\u2009300\u00a0kms<sup>\u22121</sup>) galaxy is \u223c2\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>9</sup>\u2009M<sub>\u2299</sub> (van den Bosch <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib44\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib44\" data-open=\"bib44\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>), i.e. \u223c2\u00a0per\u00a0cent of the lensing mass. We do not apply a correction for these effects.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The lens aperture luminosity is calculated from the PSF corrected <em>i</em><sub>Ein</sub>, with additional corrections for the line-of-sight galactic extinction and the <em>k-</em>correction.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Despite being located at fairly high galactic latitude (<em>b</em>\u00a0=\u00a0\u221238\u00b0), J0403 lies in a region of relatively high galactic extinction, with <em>A<sub>i</sub></em>\u00a0=\u00a00.26 according to the Schlafly &amp; Finkbeiner (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib34\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib34\" data-open=\"bib34\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>). Alternatively, maps based on PS1 stellar photometry (Schlafly et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Green et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>) indicate slightly smaller values, with <em>A<sub>i</sub></em>\u00a0=\u00a00.22. The MUSE spectrum shows a clear galactic Na\u2009D absorption doublet (\u223c1\u2009\u00c5 equivalent width), which supports the presence of substantial interstellar material along this sightline. We adopt a correction of 0.24\u2009mag, and allocate an error of 0.04\u2009mag (16\u2009per\u00a0cent of the extinction in magnitudes, following Schlegel, Finkbeiner &amp; Davis (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib36\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib36\" data-open=\"bib36\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1998</a>). The <em>k</em>-correction is estimated from the lens <em>g</em>\u2013<em>i</em> colour index (1.7) to be <em>k<sub>i</sub></em>\u00a0=\u00a00.04\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.01\u2009mag (Chilingarian, Melchior &amp; Zolotukhin <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Chilingarian &amp; Zolotukhin <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The final corrected Einstein-aperture apparent magnitude is <em>i</em><sub>Ein</sub>\u00a0=\u00a015.87\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.05, which for the adopted cosmology (<em>D</em><sub>L</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0295.7\u2009Mpc), and the solar AB <em>i</em>-band absolute magnitude of 4.534 (Blanton &amp; Roweis <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>), yields an aperture luminosity of 2.59\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.12 \u00d7 10<sup>10</sup>\u2009L<sub>\u2299</sub>. The uncertainty is dominated by the applied corrections. Combined with <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$M^*_{\\rm Ein}$|</span> the observed stellar mass-to-light ratio is \u03d2\u00a0=\u00a03.33\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.26 solar units.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">To convert from mass-to-light ratio (\u03d2) to the IMF mass factor \u03b1, we need an estimate for the reference mass-to-light ratio for a given fiducial IMF (here Kroupa <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>), which depends on the stellar population properties: age (or star formation history), metallicity, etc. To estimate these parameters, we make use of the MUSE spectrum of the lens galaxy extracted within the Einstein aperture, shown in Fig.<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a> (red line). However, the presence of significant nebular emission, combined with limited spectral coverage in the blue, presents some challenges. Specifically, the stellar population age is constrained only by the H\u2009\u03b1 and H\u2009\u03b2 absorption lines, but both are contaminated by gas emission, to different extent.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We tackle the nebular emission by first fitting a three-Gaussian model to the H\u2009\u03b1 and [N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] lines, with an assumed stellar continuum model. The relative amplitudes of the [N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] lines are fixed, and all lines are assumed to have the same velocity and width. The amplitudes of H\u2009\u03b1 and the [N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] doublet are allowed to vary. From this model, we can predict the H\u2009\u03b2 emission line, assuming Case B recombination for the intrinsic line ratio, and a relative attenuation factor of 1.135 for H\u2009\u03b2 from the MW extinction (we assume no internal extinction for this exercise). Because the underlying H\u2009\u03b1 absorption has only low sensitivity to the stellar population age, the emission correction is quite robust against changes to the age assumed in the first step.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">After making this correction to remove the emission-infilling of the spectrum at H\u2009\u03b2, we perform a full-spectrum fit over the interval 4500\u20136400\u2009\u00c5, using single-burst models from Conroy &amp; van Dokkum (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012b</a>), (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>, green). We allow for variation in abundances of Mg, Fe, Na, and C, as well as variation in age. Formally, the fit implies an old population, with age 12.0\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01.0\u2009Gyr, high metallicity [Z/H]\u2009\u2248\u2009[Mg/H]\u2009\u2248\u2009+0.15\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.02 and typical massive elliptical galaxy abundance ratios [Mg/Fe]\u2009\u2248\u2009+0.3, [Na/Fe]\u2009\u2248\u2009+0.5, and [C/Fe]\u2009\u2248\u2009+0.2.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The reference mass-to-light ratio is estimated with Conroy, Gunn &amp; White (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>) stellar population models accessed via <span class=\"small-caps\">ezgal</span> (Mancone &amp; Gonzalez <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib24\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib24\" data-open=\"bib24\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>). For a Kroupa (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2001</a>) IMF, with an old, solar metallicity stellar population (<em>z</em><sub>form</sub>\u00a0=\u00a03), the models predict <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\Upsilon ^{\\rm *}_{\\rm Ref}$|</span>\u00a0=\u00a02.90\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.10. The uncertainty is derived from small variations in the metallicity and age (e.g. <em>z</em><sub>form</sub>=\u00a02.5\u20133.5, metallicity 1\u20131.5 solar). The resulting IMF mass excess parameter, with its statistical error, is \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a01.15\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.10.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Several adopted parameters and assumptions affect the value of \u03b1 derived above. If we had prescribed the Planck Collaboration XIII (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib31\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib31\" data-open=\"bib31\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2016</a>) cosmology, \u03b1 would be 4\u2009per\u00a0cent lower, due to the variation in <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>. If we had attributed the total lensing mass entirely to the stellar component (i.e. no DM inside <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub>), we would have found \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a01.42, a 25\u2009per\u00a0cent increase. If we had assumed, due to the limited blue (most age sensitive) coverage from MUSE, that the population may be 8.5\u2009Gyr old (<em>z</em><sub>form</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01.5), \u03b1 would have increased by 14\u2009per\u00a0cent. If we had adopted the Maraston (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>) models, \u03b1 would increase by 10\u00a0per\u00a0cent.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=117976034 id=\"117976034\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec5>5 DISCUSSION AND CONCLUSIONS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We have discovered a new low-redshift gravitational lens, with a bright background galaxy, and a small Einstein radius, probing the stellar-dominated core of a massive elliptical galaxy. We have measured the lensing mass and the <em>i</em>-band Einstein aperture luminosity to estimate the IMF mismatch parameter, \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a01.15\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.10. We attribute a further uncertainty of (\u223c15\u00a0per\u00a0cent) \u00b1\u00a00.17 for the systematics. This measurement hence favours a lightweight (MW-like) IMF, rather than a heavy (e.g. Salpeter) one.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In comparison to the SNELLS systems, J0403 is more distant (<em>z</em><sub>lens</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.066 versus 0.031\u20130.052), but the background source is at much lower redshift (<em>z</em><sub>src</sub>=\u00a00.19 versus 0.93\u20132.14). As a result, the Einstein radius is smaller in angular terms (1.5\u00a0arcsec versus 2.2\u20132.9\u00a0arcsec). Thus despite the greater lens distance, <em>R</em><sub>Ein</sub> projects to a similar radius in physical units (1.8\u00a0kpc versus 1.5\u20132.2\u00a0kpc) or in galaxy-scale units (0.25<em>R</em><sub>eff</sub> versus 0.3\u20130.7<em>\u00a0R</em><sub>eff</sub>). Like the SNELLS galaxies, J0403 has high velocity dispersion and metal abundances typical for massive elliptical galaxies. Furthermore, the lensing aperture mass is compatible with the SNELLS results, favouring an MW-like IMF, and inconsistent with very bottom-heavy IMFs.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Combining our estimate of \u03b1 for J0403 with the three estimates from SNELLS (taking the values from Newman et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>; Collier et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>, i.e. SNL-0, SNL-1, SNL-2\u00a0=\u00a01.05\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.09, 1.17\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.09, 0.96\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.10), as shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>, we can infer limits on the <em>intrinsic</em> distribution of this quantity among \u03c3\u2009\u2248\u2009300\u00a0km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup> ETGs. We compute the joint likelihood of the four \u03b1 measurements as a function of the unknown population mean \u27e8\u03b1\u27e9 and dispersion \u03c3<sub>int</sub>, accounting for the measurement errors. Marginalizing over \u03c3<sub>int</sub>, with a flat prior, we infer \u27e8\u03b1\u27e9\u00a0=\u00a01.09\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.08 (the error is larger than for a calculation which assumes no intrinsic dispersion). Marginalizing over the mean, we infer only an upper limit on the scatter, with \u03c3<sub>int</sub>\u00a0\u2009&lt;\u00a0\u20090.32 at 90\u00a0per\u00a0cent confidence. (For comparison the same treatment applied to SNELLS alone yields an upper limit of \u03c3<sub>int</sub>\u00a0\u2009&lt;\u00a0\u20090.7, highlighting the impact of adding just one new measurement to the analysis.)</p>                        <a id=\"117976038\" scrollto-destination=\"117976038\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig4\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 4.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/478/2/10.1093_mnras_sty1188/2/m_sty1188fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611465988&amp;Signature=QYBxsBxr34mqwH9Vai5p2dBypXOo18M9Atu8Y0EZHRneVC3KKuaA5GohU4JKSolpoDq4qi-hST4Gx91S1k3CrpPPYrLv3jLO7IdNNgqq5naZA7kVxfHdZyvcz2p4khUoojPl8GcfjvJV9H2yiSxCckx53JWwLa7qBmHUd6VL2Mex4-3nkVBxW7IHBPt-islyzrawyaZqmbRAAKrJrODFDap1xfgV4Q5rY7l7L4CZRBM~fuMbZhXm5fnRAdrQ9g~9tVFUD5KPvu2bMaKZUd47PKYpl0~JB1su6vIsgsNSJdJlvlqKqz1Ile80Qvxi1Y34VJva-Qb-0mZLTkPRJYGcNg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The distribution of the mass excess parameter (\u03b1) for the combined SNELLS and J0403 sample. In blue, is the SNELLS sample with results for SNL-0 from Newman et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>), and SNL-1, SNL-2 from Collier et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>). The result for J0403 from this paper is marked in red. The sample average is \u27e8\u03b1\u27e9\u00a0=\u00a01.09\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.08, with an inferred <em>intrinsic</em> scatter of &lt;\u20090.32, at 90\u00a0per\u00a0cent confidence. These galaxies on average favour an MW-like IMF in preference to a Salpeter or heavier IMF.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">One important difference between J0403 and the SNELLS sample, which is relevant for future work, is that the J0403 arcs are both very bright and appear to be quite extended. Hence among all of the known low-redshift lenses, this system is uniquely suitable for pixelized lens inversion methods, which can yield much more powerful constraints on the mass distribution (e.g. Nightingale, Dye &amp; Massey <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib27\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib27\" data-open=\"bib27\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>; Oldham et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib29\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib29\" data-open=\"bib29\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2017</a>). Upcoming observations with <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> will provide high-resolution images for use in such studies.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">J0403 is by far the brightest lens system so far identified from our MUSE archival study. The results from the full sample, which is sensitive to much fainter background sources, will be the subject of a future paper (Collier et\u00a0al. in preparation). We have also begun a programme to acquire targeted MUSE observations for selected high-likelihood lenses. The discovery of the J0403 system bodes well for the success of such searches. Together with efforts elsewhere (e.g. Talbot et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib42\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib42\" data-open=\"bib42\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2018</a>), we anticipate exploiting meaningful samples of lenses at <em>z</em> \u2009\u2272\u2009 0.1 in the near future, to address the continuing puzzle of IMF variations in massive elliptical galaxies.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=117976041 id=\"117976041\" class=\"backacknowledgements-title\" >ACKNOWLEDGEMENTS</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">WPC was supported by an STFC studentship (ST/N50404X/1). RJS and JRL are supported by the STFC Durham Astronomy Consolidated Grant (ST/P000541/1). This research has made use of the NASA/IPAC Extragalactic Database (NED) which is operated by the Jet Propulsion Laboratory, California Institute of Technology, under contract with the National Aeronautics and Space Administration. This work is based on observations collected at the European Organization for Astronomical Research in the Southern hemisphere under ESO programme 098.D-0115(A), retrieved through the ESO Science Archive Facility. "Scanned", "Action": "Scanned link"}}, "Tables status": "Empty", "Paper Overview": {"type_of_reference": "JOUR", "Title": "A new strong-lensing galaxy at z= 0.066: another elliptical galaxy with a lightweight IMF", "authors": ["Collier, William P.", "Smith, Russell J.", "Lucey, John R."], "author_address": ["AA(Centre for Extragalactic Astronomy, Department of Physics, University of Durham, Durham DH1 3LE, UK), AB(Centre for Extragalactic Astronomy, Department of Physics, University of Durham, Durham DH1 3LE, UK), AC(Centre for Extragalactic Astronomy, Department of Physics, University of Durham, Durham DH1 3LE, UK)"], "journal_name": "Monthly Notices of the Royal Astronomical Society", "volume": "478", "publication_year": "2018/08/1", "start_page": "1595", "end_page": "1600", "keywords": ["gravitational lensing: strong", "galaxies: elliptical and lenticular", "cD", "galaxies: stellar content", "Astrophysics - Astrophysics of Galaxies"], "url": "https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2018MNRAS.478.1595C", "abstract": "We report the discovery of a new low-redshift galaxy-scale gravitational lens, identified from a systematic search of publicly available MUSE observations. The lens galaxy, 2MASXJ04035024-0239275, is a giant elliptical at z= 0.066 04 with a velocity dispersion of \u03c3 = 314 km s<SUP>-1</SUP>. The lensed source has a redshift of 0.191 65 and forms a pair of bright images on either side of the lens centre. The Einstein radius is 1.5 arcsec, projecting to 1.8 kpc, which is just one quarter of the galaxy effective radius. After correcting for an estimated 19 per cent dark matter contribution, we find that the stellar mass-to-light ratio from lensing is consistent with that expected for a Milky Way initial mass function (IMF). Combining the new system with three previously studied low-redshift lenses of similar \u03c3, the derived mean mass excess factor (relative to a Kroupa IMF) is \u27e8\u03b1\u27e9 = 1.09 \u00b1 0.08. With all four systems, the intrinsic scatter in \u03b1 for massive elliptical galaxies can be limited to &lt;0.32, at 90 per cent confidence.", "doi": "10.1093/mnras/sty1188", "custom1": "eprint: arXiv:1803.07082", "issn": "0035-8711", "number": "2"}}
